<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Pet Sounds</i></h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p><i><b>Pet Sounds</b></i> is the eleventh <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studio_album" title="Studio album">studio album</a> by American <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a> band <a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">the Beach Boys</a>, released on May 16, 1966. It initially met with a lukewarm critical and commercial response in the United States, peaking at number 10 in the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>, a significantly lower placement than the band's preceding albums. In the United Kingdom, the album was hailed by its music press and was an immediate commercial success, peaking at number 2 in the <a href="/wiki/UK_Albums_Chart" title="UK Albums Chart">UK Top 40 Albums Chart</a> and remaining among the top ten positions for six months. <i>Pet Sounds</i> has subsequently gathered worldwide acclaim from critics and musicians alike, and is widely considered to be one of the most influential albums in music history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fordham2016_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fordham2016-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The album was produced and <a href="/wiki/Arrangement" title="Arrangement">arranged</a> by <a href="/wiki/Brian_Wilson" title="Brian Wilson">Brian Wilson</a>, who also wrote and composed almost all of its music. Most of the recording sessions were conducted between January and April 1966, a year after he had quit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concert_tour" title="Concert tour">touring</a> with the Beach Boys in order to focus more attention on writing and recording. For <i>Pet Sounds</i>, Wilson's goal was to create "the greatest rock album ever made" — a personalized work with no <a href="/wiki/Filler_(media)" title="Filler (media)">filler tracks</a>. It is sometimes considered a Wilson solo album, repeating the themes and ideas he had introduced with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys_Today!" title="The Beach Boys Today!">The Beach Boys Today!</a></i> one year earlier. The album's <a href="/wiki/Lead_single" title="Lead single">lead single</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Caroline,_No" title="Caroline, No">Caroline, No</a>", was issued as his official solo debut. It was followed by two singles credited to the group: "<a href="/wiki/Wouldn%27t_It_Be_Nice" title="Wouldn't It Be Nice">Wouldn't It Be Nice</a>" (backed with "<a href="/wiki/God_Only_Knows" title="God Only Knows">God Only Knows</a>") and "<a href="/wiki/Sloop_John_B" title="Sloop John B">Sloop John B</a>".</p>
<p>Collaborating with lyricist <a href="/wiki/Tony_Asher" title="Tony Asher">Tony Asher</a>, Wilson's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symphonic" title="Symphonic">symphonic</a> arrangements wove elaborate layers of <a href="/wiki/Vocal_harmony" title="Vocal harmony">vocal harmonies</a>, coupled with <a href="/wiki/Sound_effect" title="Sound effect">sound effects</a> and unusual instruments such as <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_bell" title="Bicycle bell">bicycle bells</a>, buzzing <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichords</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flutes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electro-Theremin" title="Electro-Theremin">Electro-Theremin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Train" title="Train">trains</a>, Hawaiian-sounding <a href="/wiki/String_instrument" title="String instrument">string instruments</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> cans, and barking <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a>, along with the more usual keyboards and guitars. Unified by <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">Wall of Sound</a>-style production techniques, the album comprised Wilson's "pet sounds", consisting mainly of introspective songs like "<a href="/wiki/You_Still_Believe_in_Me" title="You Still Believe in Me">You Still Believe in Me</a>", about <a href="/wiki/Faithfulness" title="Faithfulness">faithfulness</a>, "<a href="/wiki/I_Know_There%27s_an_Answer" title="I Know There's an Answer">I Know There's an Answer</a>", a critique of <a href="/wiki/Lysergic_acid_diethylamide" title="Lysergic acid diethylamide">LSD</a> users, and "<a href="/wiki/I_Just_Wasn%27t_Made_for_These_Times" title="I Just Wasn't Made for These Times">I Just Wasn't Made for These Times</a>", an autobiographical statement on <a href="/wiki/Social_alienation" title="Social alienation">social alienation</a> (as well as the first use of a <a href="/wiki/Theremin" title="Theremin">theremin</a>-like instrument on a rock record). Recording was completed on April 13, 1966, with an unprecedented total production cost that exceeded $70,000 (equivalent to $520,000 in 2016). A follow-up album, <i><a href="/wiki/Smile_(The_Beach_Boys_album)" title="Smile (The Beach Boys album)">Smile</a></i>, was immediately planned, but left unfinished. In 1997, a "<a href="/wiki/Making-of" title="Making-of">making-of</a>" version of <i>Pet Sounds</i> was supervised by Wilson and released as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pet_Sounds_Sessions" title="The Pet Sounds Sessions">The Pet Sounds Sessions</a></i>, containing the album's first <a href="/wiki/Stereophonic_sound" title="Stereophonic sound">true stereo mix</a>.</p>
<p><i>Pet Sounds</i> is regarded by musicologists as an early <a href="/wiki/Concept_album" title="Concept album">concept album</a> that advanced the field of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Music_production" title="Music production">music production</a>, introducing non-standard harmonies and <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">timbres</a>, and incorporating elements of <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exotica" title="Exotica">exotica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde_music" title="Avant-garde music">avant-garde</a>. A heralding work of <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a>, the album furthered an aesthetic trend within rock by helping it transform from <a href="/wiki/Dance_music" title="Dance music">dance music</a> into music that was made for listening to, elevating itself to the level of <a href="/wiki/Art_rock" title="Art rock">art rock</a>. Author <a href="/wiki/Bill_Martin_(philosophy)" title="Bill Martin (philosophy)">Bill Martin</a> said that <i>Pet Sounds</i> represents a turning point where the Beach Boys "brought expansions in harmony, instrumentation (and therefore timbre), <a href="/wiki/Duration_(music)" title="Duration (music)">duration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhythm" title="Rhythm">rhythm</a>, and the use of <a href="/wiki/History_of_multitrack_recording" title="History of multitrack recording">recording technology</a>. Of these elements, the first and last were the most important in clearing a pathway toward the development of <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">progressive rock</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin199840_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin199840-3">[3]</a></sup> In 2004, <i>Pet Sounds</i> was preserved in the <a href="/wiki/National_Recording_Registry" title="National Recording Registry">National Recording Registry</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> for being "culturally, historically, or aesthetically significant."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Congress_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Congress-4">[4]</a></sup> One year earlier, <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> ranked it second on its list of the "<a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone%27s_500_Greatest_Albums_of_All_Time" title="Rolling Stone's 500 Greatest Albums of All Time">500 Greatest Albums of All Time</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rollingstone.com_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-rollingstone.com-5">[5]</a></sup> As of 2015, <a href="/wiki/Acclaimed_Music" title="Acclaimed Music">Acclaimed Music</a> lists <i>Pet Sounds</i> as the most statistically acclaimed album of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RacismPM_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-RacismPM-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Live_performances"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Live performances</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Release_history"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Release history</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Track_listing"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Track listing</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Personnel"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Personnel</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Charts"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Charts</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Accolades"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Accolades</span></a></li>

</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="578" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/220px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/330px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/440px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Beach Boys performing "<a href="/wiki/I_Get_Around" title="I Get Around">I Get Around</a>" on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ed_Sullivan_Show" title="The Ed Sullivan Show">The Ed Sullivan Show</a></i> in September 1964, three months before Wilson's resignation from touring</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The July 1964 release of the Beach Boys' sixth <a href="/wiki/Album" title="Album">album</a> <i><a href="/wiki/All_Summer_Long_(album)" title="All Summer Long (album)">All Summer Long</a></i> marked an end to the group's beach-themed period. From there on, their recorded material took a significantly different stylistic and lyrical path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdanovWoodstraErlewine200272.E2.80.9373_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdanovWoodstraErlewine200272.E2.80.9373-7">[7]</a></sup> While on a December 23 flight from Los Angeles to Houston, the band's songwriter and producer <a href="/wiki/Brian_Wilson" title="Brian Wilson">Brian Wilson</a> suffered a <a href="/wiki/Panic_attack" title="Panic attack">panic attack</a> only hours after performing with the group on the <a href="/wiki/Variety_show" title="Variety show">musical variety series</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Shindig!" title="Shindig!">Shindig!</a></i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez201463.E2.80.9364_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez201463.E2.80.9364-8">[8]</a></sup> The 22-year-old Wilson had already skipped several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concert_tour" title="Concert tour">concert tours</a> by then, but the airplane episode proved devastating to his psyche.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200653_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200653-9">[9]</a></sup> In order to focus his efforts on writing and recording, Wilson indefinitely resigned from live performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez201463.E2.80.9364_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez201463.E2.80.9364-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200659_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200659-10">[10]</a></sup> Freed from the burden, he immediately showcased great artistic leaps in his musical development evident within the albums <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys_Today!" title="The Beach Boys Today!">Today!</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Summer_Days_(And_Summer_Nights!!)" title="Summer Days (And Summer Nights!!)">Summer Days (And Summer Nights!!)</a></i>, released in the first half of 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007111.E2.80.93112_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007111.E2.80.93112-11">[11]</a></sup> With the July 1965 single "<a href="/wiki/California_Girls" title="California Girls">California Girls</a>", Wilson began experimenting with song composition while under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_drug" title="Psychedelic drug">psychedelic drugs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200665_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200665-12">[12]</a></sup> a factor that yielded a great effect on the group's musical conceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoward200458_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoward200458-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[nb 1]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the suggestion of bandmate <a href="/wiki/Al_Jardine" title="Al Jardine">Al Jardine</a>, Wilson began working on a track, "<a href="/wiki/Sloop_John_B" title="Sloop John B">Sloop John B</a>", which was originally a traditional <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> folk song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jardine1997_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jardine1997-19">[18]</a></sup> Wilson recorded a backing track on July 12, 1965, but after laying down a rough lead vocal, he set the song aside for some time, concentrating on the recording of what became their next LP, the informal studio jam <i><a href="/wiki/Beach_Boys%27_Party!" title="Beach Boys' Party!">Beach Boys' Party!</a></i>, in response to their record company's request for a Beach Boys album for the Christmas 1965 market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200666.E2.80.9367_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200666.E2.80.9367-20">[19]</a></sup> Wilson devoted the last three months of 1965 to polishing the vocals of "Sloop John B" and recording six new original compositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[nb 2]</a></sup> In November 1965, "<a href="/wiki/The_Little_Girl_I_Once_Knew" title="The Little Girl I Once Knew">The Little Girl I Once Knew</a>" was released as a non-album single. A section of the song features total silence, leading to poor airplay where radio stations preferred not to have moments of dead air in the middle of a song. It would be the last original Beach Boys song issued before any <i>Pet Sounds</i> tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeafTodaySummerDays_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeafTodaySummerDays-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_partnership">Writing partnership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Writing partnership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1965, Wilson met <a href="/wiki/Tony_Asher" title="Tony Asher">Tony Asher</a> at a recording studio in Los Angeles. Asher was at the time a 26-year-old lyricist and copywriter working in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingles" title="Jingles">jingles</a> for an <a href="/wiki/Advertising_agency" title="Advertising agency">advertising agency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup> The two exchanged ideas for songs, and soon after, Wilson heard of Asher's writing abilities from mutual friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup> In December 1965, he proceeded to contact Asher about a possible lyric collaboration, wanting to do something "completely different" with someone he had never written with before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200676_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200676-26">[24]</a></sup> Asher accepted the offer, and within ten days, they were writing together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup> Wilson played him some of the music he had been recording and gave him a cassette containing the backing track to a piece called "In My Childhood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[nb 3]</a></sup> The result of Asher's tryout was eventually retitled "<a href="/wiki/You_Still_Believe_in_Me" title="You Still Believe in Me">You Still Believe in Me</a>", and the success of the piece convinced Wilson that Asher was the wordsmith he had been looking for.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup> When Wilson was asked why he felt Asher was the right collaborator: "Oh, a lot of reasons. One, I thought he was a cool person. Two, anybody that hung out with [my friend] Loren Schwartz was a very brainy guy, a real verbal type person. I just felt that there was something there that had to be, you know, that really had to be."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[nb 4]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>It felt like we were writing an autobiography, but oddly enough, I wouldn't limit it to Brian's autobiography ... We were working in a somewhat intimate relationship, and I didn't know him at all, so he was finding out who I was, and I was finding out who he was.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Tony Asher<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Asher explains that he and Wilson had many lengthy, intimate discussions centered around their "experiences and feelings about women and the various stages of relationships and so forth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-7"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup> He maintains that his contribution to the music itself was minimal, serving mainly as a source of second opinion for Wilson as he worked out possible melodies and chord progressions, although the two did trade ideas as the songs evolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-8"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[nb 5]</a></sup> On his role as co-lyricist, Asher clarified, "The general tenor of the lyrics was always his ... and the actual choice of words was usually mine. I was really just his interpreter."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKent200916_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKent200916-33">[28]</a></sup> While speaking about "<a href="/wiki/God_Only_Knows" title="God Only Knows">God Only Knows</a>", Wilson reflected: "I think Tony had a musical influence on me somehow. After about ten years, I started thinking about it deeper ... because I had never written that kind of song. And I remember him talking about '<a href="/wiki/Stella_by_Starlight" title="Stella by Starlight">Stella by Starlight</a>' and he had a certain love for classic songs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[nb 6]</a></sup> A typical writing session would either start with Wilson playing melody or chord patterns that he'd been working on, by discussing a recent record that Wilson liked the feel of, or by discussing a subject that Wilson had always wanted to write a song about.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-9"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>While most of the album's songs derived from the Wilson–Asher partnership, "<a href="/wiki/I_Know_There%27s_an_Answer" title="I Know There's an Answer">I Know There's an Answer</a>" was co-written by Wilson with another new associate, the Beach Boys' road manager Terry Sachen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tracks_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-36">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan1984139_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan1984139-37">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200450_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200450-38">[32]</a></sup> In 1994, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Love" title="Mike Love">Mike Love</a> was awarded co-writing credits on "<a href="/wiki/Wouldn%27t_It_Be_Nice" title="Wouldn't It Be Nice">Wouldn't It Be Nice</a>" and "I Know There's an Answer",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200922.2C_25_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200922.2C_25-39">[33]</a></sup> but with the exception of his co-credit on "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_Waiting_for_the_Day" title="I'm Waiting for the Day">I'm Waiting for the Day</a>", a song which had been written some two years earlier,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tracks_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-36">[30]</a></sup> his songwriting contributions are thought to have been minimal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tracks_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-36">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[nb 7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept_and_inspiration">Concept and inspiration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Concept and inspiration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MFQ_with_Phil_Spector.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="393" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/MFQ_with_Phil_Spector.jpg/220px-MFQ_with_Phil_Spector.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/MFQ_with_Phil_Spector.jpg/330px-MFQ_with_Phil_Spector.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/05/MFQ_with_Phil_Spector.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MFQ_with_Phil_Spector.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Phil_Spector" title="Phil Spector">Phil Spector</a> (center) at <a href="/wiki/Gold_Star_Studios" title="Gold Star Studios">Gold Star Studios</a>, where he developed his <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">Wall of Sound</a> methods, 1966</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Commentators and historians frequently cite <i>Pet Sounds</i> as a <a href="/wiki/Concept_album" title="Concept album">concept album</a> — the first of rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELambert2007249_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELambert2007249-41">[34]</a></sup> Author Carys Wyn Jones attribute this to the album's "uniform excellence" rather than a lyrical theme or musical motif.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones200844_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones200844-42">[35]</a></sup> Even though <i>Pet Sounds</i> has a somewhat unified theme in its emotional content, there wasn't a predetermined narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETunbridge2010173_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETunbridge2010173-43">[36]</a></sup> Asher said that there were no conversations between him and Wilson that pertained to any specific album "concept", however, "that's not to say that he didn't have the capacity to steer it in that direction, even unconsciously".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-10"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup> Wilson stated: "If you take the <i>Pet Sounds</i> album as a collection of art pieces, each designed to stand alone, yet which belong together, you'll see what I was aiming at. ... It wasn't really a <i>song</i> concept album, or <i>lyrically</i> a concept album; it was really a <i>production</i> concept album."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETunbridge2010173.E2.80.93174_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETunbridge2010173.E2.80.93174-44">[37]</a></sup> He added that the album may be considered an "interpretation" of <a href="/wiki/Phil_Spector" title="Phil Spector">Phil Spector</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">Wall of Sound</a> recording methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>For <i>Pet Sounds</i>, Wilson desired to make "a complete statement", similar to what he believed <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a> had done with their newest album <i><a href="/wiki/Rubber_Soul" title="Rubber Soul">Rubber Soul</a></i> (December 1965).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones200844_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones200844-42">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[nb 8]</a></sup> Wilson was immediately enamored with the album, given the impression that it had no <a href="/wiki/Filler_(media)" title="Filler (media)">filler tracks</a>, a feature that was mostly unheard of at a time when <a href="/wiki/Single_(music)" title="Single (music)">45 rpm singles</a> were considered more noteworthy than full-length <a href="/wiki/LP_record" title="LP record">LPs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580-49">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114-50">[42]</a></sup> Many albums up until the mid-1960s lacked a cohesive artistic goal and were largely used to sell singles at a higher price point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580-49">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[nb 9]</a></sup> Wilson found that <i>Rubber Soul</i> subverted this by having a wholly consistent thread of music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580-49">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114-50">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[nb 10]</a></sup> Inspired, he rushed to his wife and proclaimed, "<a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Wilson-Rutherford" title="Marilyn Wilson-Rutherford">Marilyn</a>, I'm gonna make the greatest album! The greatest rock album ever made!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200675_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200675-55">[45]</a></sup> He would say of his reaction to <i>Rubber Soul</i>: "I liked the way it all went together, the way it was all one thing. It was a challenge to me ... It didn't make me want to copy them but to be as good as them. I didn't want to do the same kind of music, but on the same level."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HimesSurf_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-HimesSurf-56">[46]</a></sup> Later, he clarified: "The Beatles <i>inspired</i> me. They didn't influence me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[nb 11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Author Michael Zager wrote that <i>Pet Sounds</i> resembles Spector's productions more than it does <i>Rubber Soul</i>, and that it recycles many of Spector's Wall of Sound production watermarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZager2012218_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZager2012218-61">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[nb 12]</a></sup> Wilson talked of Spector's influence on his work, having learned how to produce records through attending his sessions, and in a 1988 interview, Wilson asserted that the album aspired to "extend" Spector's music, that "in one sense of the word, we [the Beach Boys] were his messengers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELambert2007225_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELambert2007225-58">[48]</a></sup> In <i>Pet Sounds</i>, Wilson sought to emulate Spector's Wall of Sound, driven by his fascination with "combining one [sound] to make another ... It's amazing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoorefield201016.E2.80.9317_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoorefield201016.E2.80.9317-64">[52]</a></sup> Shortly before the album's release, Wilson spoke of recent popular music trends, saying "[They] helped the Beach Boys evolve. We listen to what's happening and it affects what we do too. The trends have influenced my work, but so has my own scene."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MelodyMakerBlast_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-MelodyMakerBlast-65">[53]</a></sup> In 1996, Wilson said of his partnership with Asher: "none of us looked back ... We went forward, kind of like on our own little wavelength. It wasn't like we were thinking, 'Okay, let's beat Spector,' let's out-do <a href="/wiki/Motown" title="Motown">Motown</a>.' It was more what I would call exclusive collaboration not to specifically try to kick somebody's butt, but just to do it the way you really want it to be. That's what I thought we did."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[nb 13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Group_infighting">Group infighting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Group infighting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Pet Sounds</i> is sometimes considered a Brian Wilson solo album in all but name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoskyns2009106_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoskyns2009106-68">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdanovWoodstraErlewine200272_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdanovWoodstraErlewine200272-69">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200920_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200920-70">[57]</a></sup> He explained: "<i>Pet Sounds</i> was something that was absolutely different. Something I personally felt. That one album that was really more me than Mike Love and the surf records and all that, and '<a href="/wiki/Kokomo_(song)" title="Kokomo (song)">Kokomo</a>'. That's all their kind of stuff, you know?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup> When the other Beach Boys returned from a three-week tour of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, they were presented with a substantial portion of a new album, with music that was in many ways a jarring departure from their earlier style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaines2009149_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaines2009149-71">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[nb 14]</a></sup> According to various reports, the group fought over the new direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKent200921.E2.80.9323_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKent200921.E2.80.9323-75">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Wilson" title="Dennis Wilson">Dennis Wilson</a> denied the reports: "That's interesting. There's not one person in the group that could come close to Brian's talent, and I couldn't imagine who the fight would be."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the issues was the album's complexity and how the touring Beach Boys would be able to perform its music live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-danny_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-danny-77">[63]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (February 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Wilson said that the band "didn't like the idea of growing musically ... They wanted to keep making <a href="/wiki/Car_song" title="Car song">car songs</a> and I said 'No, we’ve gotta grow, guys'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins2016_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins2016-78">[64]</a></sup> Marilyn said: "When Brian was writing <i>Pet Sounds</i>, it was difficult for the guys to understand what he was going through emotionally and what he wanted to create. ... they didn't feel what he was going through and what direction he was trying to go in."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marilyn1997_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marilyn1997-79">[65]</a></sup> Tony Asher remembered: "All those guys in the band, certainly Al, Dennis, and Mike, were constantly saying, 'What the fuck do these words mean?' or 'This isn't our kind of shit!' Brian had comebacks, though. He'd say, 'Oh, you guys can't hack this.' ... But I remember thinking that those were tense sessions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200683_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200683-80">[66]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:166px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mike_Love_in_1966.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="153" data-file-width="164" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Mike_Love_in_1966.gif" width="164"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mike_Love_in_1966.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Love dismissed claims which say he disliked the album, calling them "a bunch of bullshit",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[67]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mike Love is quoted to have said "some of the [<i>Pet Sounds</i> lyrics] were so offensive that I wouldn't even sing 'em".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrvin200764_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrvin200764-82">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[nb 15]</a></sup> The original lyrics of "I Know There's an Answer", known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hang_On_to_Your_Ego" title="Hang On to Your Ego">Hang On to Your Ego</a>", created a stir within the group. He opposed the song's original message, though Jardine recalled that the decision to change the lyrics was ultimately Brian's: "Brian was very concerned. He wanted to know what we thought about it. To be honest, I don't think we even knew what an ego was ... Finally Brian decided, 'Forget it. I'm changing the lyrics. There's too much controversy.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tracks_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-36">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[nb 16]</a></sup> On Love's reaction to the album, Jardine commented: "Mike was very confused ... Mike's a formula hound – if it doesn't have a hook in it, if he can't hear a hook in it, he doesn't want to know about it. ... I wasn't exactly thrilled with the change, but I grew to really appreciate it as soon as we started to work on it. It wasn't like anything we'd heard before."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine2000_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine2000-87">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilson" title="Carl Wilson">Carl Wilson</a> said: "I loved every minute of it. He [Brian] could do no wrong. He could play me anything, and I would love it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carl1997_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carl1997-88">[72]</a></sup> Brian remembers that Carl was enthused with the album, but not Dennis and Mike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilsonGreenman2016_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilsonGreenman2016-89">[73]</a></sup> Author John Tobler wrote that Dennis and <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Johnston" title="Bruce Johnston">Bruce Johnston</a> loved the album, but that Jardine felt "It sure doesn't sound like the old stuff."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200919_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200919-90">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brian believed the band was worried about him separating from the group, elaborating that "it was generally considered that the Beach Boys were the main thing ... with <i>Pet Sounds</i>, there was a resistance in that I was doing most of the artistic work on it vocally".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunch76_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lunch76-91">[75]</a></sup> Love wrote: "I also would have liked to have had a greater hand in some of the songs and been able to incorporate more often my 'lead voice,' which we’d had so much success with."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELove2016135_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELove2016135-92">[76]</a></sup> The conflicts were resolved, according to Brian, "[when] they figured that it was a showcase for Brian Wilson, but it's still the Beach Boys. In other words, they gave in. They let me have my little stint."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunch76_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lunch76-91">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_and_lyrics">Music and lyrics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Music and lyrics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="mbox-small-left" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;float:left">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">

</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">

<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">"<a href="/wiki/Wouldn%27t_It_Be_Nice" title="Wouldn't It Be Nice">Wouldn't It Be Nice</a>" introduces the album with a sound described by <a href="/wiki/Nick_Kent" title="Nick Kent">Nick Kent</a> as "limpid harps imitating a teenage heartstrings in a tug of love" with it followed by "growling horns ... [and] harmonies so complex they seemed to have more in common with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Mass" title="Catholic Mass">Catholic Mass</a> than any cocktail lounge acappella <a href="/wiki/Doo-wop" title="Doo-wop">doo-wop</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKent200917_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKent200917-93">[77]</a></sup></div>
</div>

</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><i>Pet Sounds</i> is often considered within the canon of <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixDegrees_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-SixDegrees-94">[78]</a></sup> such as by historian Susie Skarl<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmondson2013104_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmondson2013104-95">[79]</a></sup> and music journalist <a href="/wiki/Jim_DeRogatis" title="Jim DeRogatis">Jim DeRogatis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis2003xi_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis2003xi-96">[80]</a></sup> whereas "<a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_pop" title="Psychedelic pop">psychedelic pop</a>" was identified by <i><a href="/wiki/Goldmine_(magazine)" title="Goldmine (magazine)">Goldmine</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoldminePsyche_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoldminePsyche-97">[81]</a></sup> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Journal_Sentinel" title="Journal Sentinel">Journal Sentinel</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[82]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paste_magazine" title="Paste magazine">Paste</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Bryan Rolli deemed it a "masterpiece" of <a href="/wiki/Progressive_pop" title="Progressive pop">progressive pop</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[83]</a></sup> a designation also lent by various newspapers when the album was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeaf198587.E2.80.9388_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeaf198587.E2.80.9388-100">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pc37_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-pc37-59">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez201481_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez201481-101">[85]</a></sup> It is described as "<a href="/wiki/Art_rock" title="Art rock">art rock</a>" by Jones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones200849_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones200849-102">[86]</a></sup> biographer <a href="/wiki/David_Leaf" title="David Leaf">David Leaf</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeaf198574_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeaf198574-103">[87]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Patrick Foster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[88]</a></sup> The "<a href="/wiki/Baroque_pop" title="Baroque pop">baroque pop</a>" label was given by the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avclub_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-avclub-105">[89]</a></sup> and <i>The A.V. Club</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APviaHuffPost_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-APviaHuffPost-106">[90]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Experimental_rock" title="Experimental rock">Experimental rock</a>" was offered by author Kelly Fisher Lowe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELowe2007219_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELowe2007219-107">[91]</a></sup> and <i>Music Insight</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Anni Fordham.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fordham2016_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fordham2016-2">[2]</a></sup> "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avant-pop_music" title="Avant-pop music">Avant-pop</a>" was applied by biographer <a href="/wiki/Peter_Ames_Carlin" title="Peter Ames Carlin">Peter Ames Carlin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[92]</a></sup> and writer Paul Grimstad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-br-109">[93]</a></sup> Wilson himself thought of the album as "chapel rock ... commercial choir music. I wanted to make an album that would stand up in ten years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrvin200764_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrvin200764-82">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[nb 17]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:God_Only_Knows_choral_fantasy.ogg" title="File:God Only Knows choral fantasy.ogg">The bridge of "God Only Knows"</a></div>
<div>
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="24.952199546485" data-mwprovider="local" data-mwtitle="God_Only_Knows_choral_fantasy.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_1" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="70823" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (71 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f8/God_Only_Knows_choral_fantasy.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'></source></audio></div>

<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">For the basic rhythmic feel of "<a href="/wiki/God_Only_Knows" title="God Only Knows">God Only Knows</a>", <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichord</a>, piano with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slapback_echo" title="Slapback echo">slapback echo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleigh_bell" title="Sleigh bell">sleigh bells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/String_section" title="String section">strings</a> <a href="/wiki/Spill_(audio)" title="Spill (audio)">spills</a> into each other to create a rich blanket of sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZak200188_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZak200188-115">[98]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>According to biographer <a href="/wiki/Jon_Stebbins" title="Jon Stebbins">Jon Stebbins</a>, "Brian defies any notion of genre safety".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbins2011152_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbins2011152-116">[99]</a></sup> The album's innovative <a href="/wiki/Soundscape" title="Soundscape">soundscape</a> incorporates elements of <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jazz_music" title="Jazz music">jazz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exotica" title="Exotica">exotica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde_music" title="Avant-garde music">avant-garde music</a>, as Stebbins adds, "There isn't much rocking here, and even less rolling. <i>Pet Sounds</i> is at times futuristic, progressive, and experimental. ... there's <a href="/wiki/Boogie-woogie" title="Boogie-woogie">no boogie, no woogie</a>, and the only <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a> are in the themes and in Brian's voice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbins2011151.E2.80.93152_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbins2011151.E2.80.93152-117">[100]</a></sup> In <i>Pet Sounds</i>, Wilson conceived of experimental arrangements<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201229_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201229-118">[101]</a></sup> which combine conventional rock set-ups with various exotic instruments, producing new sounds with a rich texture reminiscent of symphonic works layered underneath meticulous vocal harmonics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114-50">[42]</a></sup> Many of those instruments were alien to rock music, including <a href="/wiki/Glockenspiel" title="Glockenspiel">glockenspiel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukulele" title="Ukulele">ukulele</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accordion" title="Accordion">accordion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electro-Theremin" title="Electro-Theremin">Electro-Theremin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bongo_drum" title="Bongo drum">bongos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichord</a>, <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cello</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trombone" title="Trombone">trombone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> bottles, and other odd sounds such as barking <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Train" title="Train">trains</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_bell" title="Bicycle bell">bicycle bells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200938_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200938-119">[102]</a></sup> This kind of selection was stylistically appropriated from a wide variety of cultures, with some relating it to exotica and its associated producers <a href="/wiki/Martin_Denny" title="Martin Denny">Martin Denny</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnterbergerHicks1999469_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnterbergerHicks1999469-120">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELambert2007232_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELambert2007232-121">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbins2011152_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbins2011152-116">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Les_Baxter" title="Les Baxter">Les Baxter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELambert2007232_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELambert2007232-121">[104]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Juan_Garc%C3%ADa_Esquivel" title="Juan García Esquivel">Esquivel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitchfork2006_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pitchfork2006-122">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Journalist Alice Bolin writes that <i>Pet Sounds</i> "repeated and perfected" the themes and ideas introduced in the Beach Boys' album <i>Today!</i>, released one year earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bolin2012_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bolin2012-123">[106]</a></sup> Author Chris Smith wrote that the complex arrangements and more developed themes of <i>Today!</i> effectively foreshadowed the lush orchestration and maturity of <i>Pet Sounds</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200937_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200937-124">[107]</a></sup> Marshall Heiser expressed for <i>The Journal on the Art of Record Production</i>: "<i>Pet Sounds</i> diverges from previous Beach Boys' efforts in several ways: its sound field has a greater sense of depth and 'warmth;' the songs employ even more inventive use of harmony and chord voicings; the prominent use of percussion is a key feature (as opposed to driving drum backbeats); whilst the orchestrations, at times, echo the quirkiness of 'exotica' bandleader Les Baxter, or the 'cool' of <a href="/wiki/Burt_Bacharach" title="Burt Bacharach">Burt Bacharach</a>, more so than [Phil] Spector's teen fanfares."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARP_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARP-125">[108]</a></sup> Musicologist <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Harrison_(music_theorist)" title="Daniel Harrison (music theorist)">Daniel Harrison</a> has written: "In terms of the structure of the songs themselves, there is comparatively little advance from what Brian had already accomplished or shown himself capable of accomplishing. Most of the songs use unusual <a href="/wiki/Chord_progression" title="Chord progression">harmonic progressions</a> and unexpected disruptions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypermeter" title="Hypermeter">hypermeter</a>, both features that were met in '<a href="/wiki/The_Warmth_of_the_Sun" title="The Warmth of the Sun">Warmth of the Sun</a>' and '<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Back_Down_(song)" title="Don't Back Down (song)">Don't Back Down</a>.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovachBoone19973_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovachBoone19973-126">[109]</a></sup> Journalist <a href="/wiki/Nick_Kent" title="Nick Kent">Nick Kent</a> felt "Wouldn't It Be Nice" to be "teen angst dialogue" that Wilson had already achieved with "<a href="/wiki/We%27ll_Run_Away" title="We'll Run Away">We'll Run Away</a>" the year before. However: "This time Brian Wilson was out to eclipse these previous sonic soap operas, to transform the subject's sappy sentiments with a God-like grace so that the song would become a veritable pocket symphony."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKent200917_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKent200917-93">[77]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>

<source data-bandwidth="159701" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (160 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/44/Here_Today_Pet_Sounds.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'></source></audio></div>
