<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">"<a href="/wiki/Here_Today_(The_Beach_Boys_song)" title="Here Today (The Beach Boys song)">Here Today</a>" has been described by <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a> as one of Wilson's most ambitious arrangements, featuring "swooping harmonies [that] duel and soar in and around a <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">Wall of Sound</a> backing track full of blaring saxophones and percolating keyboards" along with "jazzy drumming" that blends the "complexity of an orchestral piece with the immediacy of a good pop tune".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[110]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>As author James Perone explains, Wilson's compositions include tempo changes, metrical ambiguity, and unusual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tone_color" title="Tone color">tone colors</a> that, culturally speaking, separates the album from any pop music of the era. It also adds to "the background tenderness and melancholy—as well as the increased maturity—that ebb and flow throughout <i>Pet Sounds</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201228_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201228-128">[111]</a></sup> He specifically touches upon the album's closer "<a href="/wiki/Caroline,_No" title="Caroline, No">Caroline, No</a>" and its use of wide <a href="/wiki/Tessitura" title="Tessitura">tessitura</a> changes, wide melodic intervals, and instrumentation which contribute to his belief; also Wilson's compositions and orchestral arrangements which experiment with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Form_(music)" title="Form (music)">form</a> and tone colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201230_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201230-129">[112]</a></sup> Referring to "Wouldn't It Be Nice", Perone recalls that the track sounds "significantly less like a rock band supplemented with auxiliary instrumentation ... than a rock band integrated into an eclectic mix of studio instrumentation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201228.2C_30_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201228.2C_30-130">[113]</a></sup> Arranger Paul Mertens believes: "There are string sections on <i>Pet Sounds</i>. But to me, what's special about that is not that Brian was trying to introduce classical music into rock &amp; roll. Rather, he was trying to get classical musicians to play like rock musicians. He's using these things to make music in the way that he understood, rather than trying to appropriate the orchestra. Often he'll direct a drummer to play in a very unconventional way, in a way that drummers don't normally play. He'll say, 'No, no, don't play the hi-hat, just go bomp...ba-domp...bomp...ba-domp on the tom and kick drum.' He's feeling a certain thing, but coming at it from a musical idea rather than the conventions of the instrument."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The album included two sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Instrumental" title="Instrumental">instrumental</a> tracks composed solely by Brian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201228_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201228-128">[111]</a></sup> One of them: the wistful "<a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Go_Away_for_Awhile" title="Let's Go Away for Awhile">Let's Go Away for Awhile</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200449_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200449-132">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[116]</a></sup> the other instrumental is the title track, "<a href="/wiki/Pet_Sounds_(instrumental)" title="Pet Sounds (instrumental)">Pet Sounds</a>". Both titles had been recorded as backing tracks for existing songs, but by the time the album neared completion Wilson had decided that the tracks worked better without vocals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianPopGenius_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrianPopGenius-134">[117]</a></sup> Of "Let's Go Away for Awhile", Perone observes, "There are melodic features but no tune to speak of. As an instrumental composition, this gives the piece an atmospheric feel; however, the exact mood is difficult to define."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201228_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201228-128">[111]</a></sup> Of "Pet Sounds", the piece represents the Beach Boys' surf heritage more than any other track on the album with its emphasis on lead guitar, although Perone maintains that it is not really a surf composition, citing its elaborate arrangement involving countless auxiliary percussion parts, abruptly changing textures, and de-emphasis of a traditional rock band drum set.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201229_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201229-118">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes">Themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manuscript_of_Lyrics_for_God_Only_Knows_-_Rock_%26_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum,_Cleveland_(by_Adam_Jones).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Manuscript_of_Lyrics_for_God_Only_Knows_-_Rock_%26_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum%2C_Cleveland_%28by_Adam_Jones%29.jpg/220px-Manuscript_of_Lyrics_for_God_Only_Knows_-_Rock_%26_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum%2C_Cleveland_%28by_Adam_Jones%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Manuscript_of_Lyrics_for_God_Only_Knows_-_Rock_%26_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum%2C_Cleveland_%28by_Adam_Jones%29.jpg/330px-Manuscript_of_Lyrics_for_God_Only_Knows_-_Rock_%26_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum%2C_Cleveland_%28by_Adam_Jones%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Manuscript_of_Lyrics_for_God_Only_Knows_-_Rock_%26_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum%2C_Cleveland_%28by_Adam_Jones%29.jpg/440px-Manuscript_of_Lyrics_for_God_Only_Knows_-_Rock_%26_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum%2C_Cleveland_%28by_Adam_Jones%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manuscript_of_Lyrics_for_God_Only_Knows_-_Rock_%26_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum,_Cleveland_(by_Adam_Jones).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Original handwritten manuscript for the lyrics to "God Only Knows"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For much of <i>Pet Sounds</i> lyrical content, Brian turned inward and probed his deep-seated self-doubts and emotional longings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis200334_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis200334-135">[118]</a></sup> However, as journalist <a href="/wiki/Liel_Leibovitz" title="Liel Leibovitz">Liel Leibovitz</a> writes, the resulting album "wasn’t Wilson’s autobiography or Asher’s but rather a collective autobiography of everything we all feel when we ... try and reach out to another human being".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lebo2016_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lebo2016-136">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[nb 18]</a></sup> Tony Asher explains:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Brian was constantly looking for topics that kids could relate to. Even though he was dealing in the most advanced score-charts and arrangements, he was still incredibly conscious of this commercial thing. This absolute need to relate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKent200917_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKent200917-93">[77]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>According to reviewer Jim Esch, the opener "Wouldn't It Be Nice" inaugurates and suggests the album's pervasive theme: "fragile lovers buckling under the pressure of external forces they can't control, self-imposed romantic expectations and personal limitations, while simultaneously trying to maintain faith in one other. It is a theme that keeps reverberating sweetly, and hauntingly, throughout <i>Pet Sounds</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YSBiMAllMusic_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-YSBiMAllMusic-139">[121]</a></sup> Carl said: "The disappointment and the loss of innocence that everyone had to go through when they grow up and find everything's not Hollywood are the recurrent themes on that album."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HimesSurf_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-HimesSurf-56">[46]</a></sup> Critics <a href="/wiki/Richard_Goldstein_(writer_born_1944)" title="Richard Goldstein (writer born 1944)">Richard Goldstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nik_Cohn" title="Nik Cohn">Nik Cohn</a> noted incongruity between the album's music and lyrics, where the latter suggested the work to be "sad songs about happiness" while also celebrating loneliness and heartache.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaines2009146_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaines2009146-140">[122]</a></sup> In the opinion of Perone, it is the next track, "You Still Believe in Me", which features the first expression of introspective themes that pervade the rest of the album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201228.2C_30_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201228.2C_30-130">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Responding to the songwriters' denials of a conscious lyric theme, Nick Kent observed: "[The] album documents the male participant's attempts at coming to terms with himself and the world about him. Each song pinpoints a crisis of faith in love and life: confusion ('<a href="/wiki/That%27s_Not_Me" title="That's Not Me">That's Not Me</a>'), disorientation (the staggeringly beautiful '<a href="/wiki/I_Just_Wasn%27t_Made_for_These_Times" title="I Just Wasn't Made for These Times">I Just Wasn't Made for These Times</a>'), recognition of love's capricious impermanence ('<a href="/wiki/Here_Today_(The_Beach_Boys_song)" title="Here Today (The Beach Boys song)">Here Today</a>') and finally, the grand betrayal of innocence featured in 'Caroline, No'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKent200923.E2.80.9324_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKent200923.E2.80.9324-141">[123]</a></sup> From another viewpoint, author Scott Schinder has written that Wilson and Asher crafted an "emotion-charge <a href="/wiki/Song_cycle" title="Song cycle">song cycle</a> that surveyed the emotional challenges accompanying the transition from youth to adulthood."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114.E2.80.93115_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114.E2.80.93115-142">[124]</a></sup> Schinder continues: "Lyrically, <i>Pet Sounds</i> encompassed the loss of innocent idealism ("Caroline, No"), the transient nature of love ("Here Today"), faith in the face of heartbreak ("I'm Waiting for the Day"), the demands and disappointments of independence ("That's Not Me"), the feeling of being out of step with the modern world ("I Just Wasn't Made for These Times"), and the longing for a happy, loving future ("Wouldn't It Be Nice"). The album also featured a series of intimate, <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymn-like</a> love songs, "You Still Believe in Me", "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Talk_(Put_Your_Head_on_My_Shoulder)" title="Don't Talk (Put Your Head on My Shoulder)">Don't Talk (Put Your Head on My Shoulder)</a>", and "God Only Knows"."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114.E2.80.93115_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114.E2.80.93115-142">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>"Then again," Kent says, "bearing in mind this conceptual bent, there are certain incongruous factors about the album's construction. The main one is the inclusion of the hit single 'Sloop John B', as well as of two instrumental pieces."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKent200923.E2.80.9324_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKent200923.E2.80.9324-141">[123]</a></sup> Perone argues, "To the extent that the listener hears 'Let's Go Away for Awhile' as an incomplete piece, it is possible to understand it as a reflection of the alienation – the sense of not quite fitting in – of the bulk of Tony Asher's lyrics in the songs on <i>Pet Sounds</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201228_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201228-128">[111]</a></sup> Noting that a sense of self-doubt, concern for the future of a relationship, and melancholy pervades <i>Pet Sounds</i>, Perone claims in reference to "Sloop John B" that the song successfully portrays a sailor who feels "completely out of place in his situation [which] is fully in keeping with the general feeling of disorientation that runs through so many of the songs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201228_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201228-128">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fussili states that Wilson's tendency to "wander far from the logic of his composition only to return triumphantly to confirm the emotional intent of his work" is repeated numerous times in <i>Pet Sounds</i>, but never to "evoke a sense of unbridled joy" as Wilson recently had with the single "The Little Girl I Once Knew".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFusilli2005_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFusilli2005-143">[125]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2014)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the example of "God Only Knows", which contains an ambivalent <a href="/wiki/Key_(music)" title="Key (music)">key</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_and_chromatic#Chords" title="Diatonic and chromatic">non-diatonic chords</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovachBoone19973_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovachBoone19973-126">[109]</a></sup> musicologist Philip Lambert cites its "choral fantasy" section to contain complex key changes that elude the listener "for the entire experience — that in fact, the idea of 'key' has itself been challenged and subverted".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELambert2007245_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELambert2007245-144">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[nb 19]</a></sup> Perone's interpretation also suggests a visceral musical continuity. On the second track "You Still Believe in Me", "One of the high points of the composition and Brian's vocal performance ... is the snaky, though generally descending melodic line on the line 'I want to cry,' his response to the realization that his girlfriend still believes in him despite his past failures." He describes the "stepwise falloff of the interval of a third at the end of each verse" to be a typically "Wilsonian" feature that recurs throughout the album in addition to a "madrigal sigh <a href="/wiki/Motif_(music)" title="Motif (music)">motif</a>" that can be heard in "That's Not Me", where the motif concludes each line of the verses. This sighing motif appears in the next track, "Don't Talk (Put Your Head on My Shoulder)", a piece inspired by classical music, and once again in "Caroline, No".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201228.2C_30_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201228.2C_30-130">[113]</a></sup> For the high-pitched electric bass guitar part in "Here Today", Perone says they bring to mind similar parts in "God Only Knows", culminating in what sounds like the vocal protagonist of "Here Today" warning the protagonist of "God Only Knows" that what he sings stands no chance at longevity. The protagonist's relationship then concludes shortly after "I Just Wasn't Made for These Times", while "Caroline, No" is a rumination in broken love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerone201229_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerone201229-118">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychedelia">Psychedelia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Psychedelia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:I_Know_There%27s_an_Answer.ogg" title="File:I Know There's an Answer.ogg">"I Know There's an Answer" sections</a></div>
<div>
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="30.002335600907" data-mwprovider="local" data-mwtitle="I_Know_There's_an_Answer.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_3" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="83087" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (83 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/94/I_Know_There%27s_an_Answer.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'></source></audio></div>
</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"><a href="/wiki/Consequence_of_Sound" title="Consequence of Sound">Consequence of Sound</a>'s Zach Ruskin expressed: "while <i>Pet Sounds</i> offers an intimacy unlike other psychedelic pop of the time, soundscapes of whispers and reverb and sudden departures in structure and form do lend the record a somewhat trippy effect."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruskin2016_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruskin2016-146">[127]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Wilson's response when asked about LSD and "Hang On to Your Ego" was: "I had taken a few drugs, and I had gotten into that kind of thing. I guess it just came up naturally."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Out-Of-Sight.21_SMiLE_Timeline_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Out-Of-Sight.21_SMiLE_Timeline-147">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[nb 20]</a></sup> Despite concerns over the song's drug references, the key lyric "they trip through the day and waste all their thoughts at night" was kept when the song was revised as "I Know There's an Answer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranata2003105_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranata2003105-150">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELambert2007237_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELambert2007237-151">[131]</a></sup> Elsewhere for "Sloop John B", Wilson's lyric change from "this is the worst trip <i>since I've been born</i>" to "this is the worst trip <i>I've ever been on</i>" has also been suggested by some to be another subtle nod to acid culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis2003_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis2003-154">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_Britannica" title="Encyclopedia Britannica">Encyclopedia Britannica</a></i> states that the Beach Boys introduced psychedelic elements with the album, calling it "expansive" and "haunting".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[135]</a></sup> Writer Vernon Joyson observes flirtations with <a href="/wiki/Acid_rock" title="Acid rock">acid rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJoyson19848_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJoyson19848-156">[136]</a></sup> Bret Marcus of <i>Goldmine</i> noted that he believes that the album is psychedelic pop, even though most people hesitate to name the Beach Boys in discussions of psychedelic music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoldminePsyche_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-GoldminePsyche-97">[81]</a></sup> Stebbins writes that the album is "slightly psychedelic – or at least impressionistic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbins2011152_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbins2011152-116">[99]</a></sup> According to academics <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hegarty_(musician)" title="Paul Hegarty (musician)">Paul Hegarty</a> and Martin Halliwell, <i>Pet Sounds</i> has a "personal intimacy" that sets it apart from the Beach Boys' contemporaries in <a href="/wiki/Psychedelia" title="Psychedelia">psychedelic culture</a> and the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Sound" title="San Francisco Sound">San Francisco Sound</a>, but still retains a "trippy feel" that resulted from Wilson's experimental use of LSD. They attribute this to Wilson's "eclectic mixture of instruments, echo, reverb, and innovative mixing techniques learnt from Phil Spector to create a complex soundscape in which voice and music interweave tightly".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHegartyHalliwell201123_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHegartyHalliwell201123-157">[137]</a></sup> Wilson acknowledged that psychedelic elements are present in a number of the songs, but believes "the album itself is mostly not psychedelic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruskin2016_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruskin2016-146">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>DeRogatis compared the album's repeated listening value to a heightened psychedelic awareness, that its melodies "continue to reveal themselves after dozens of listens, just as previously unnoticed corners of the world reveal themselves during the psychedelic experience".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis200336_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis200336-158">[138]</a></sup> On the subject of psychedelic records in the 1960s, <a href="/wiki/Sean_Lennon" title="Sean Lennon">Sean Lennon</a> stated that "psychedelic music is a term that pretty much refers to these sort of epic, ambitious long-form records ... the reason <i>Pet Sounds</i> is considered a psychedelic journey or whatever is because it's like opening a door and stepping through and entering another world and you're in that other world for a period of time and then you come back."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recording_and_production">Recording and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Recording and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">Wall of Sound</a> and <a href="/wiki/Musicianship_of_Brian_Wilson" title="Musicianship of Brian Wilson">Musicianship of Brian Wilson</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Wilson_Rickenbacker.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="351" data-file-width="478" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/46/Carl_Wilson_Rickenbacker.png/220px-Carl_Wilson_Rickenbacker.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/46/Carl_Wilson_Rickenbacker.png/330px-Carl_Wilson_Rickenbacker.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/46/Carl_Wilson_Rickenbacker.png/440px-Carl_Wilson_Rickenbacker.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Wilson_Rickenbacker.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilson" title="Carl Wilson">Carl Wilson</a> at Western Studio 3 of <a href="/wiki/United_Western_Recorders" title="United Western Recorders">United Western Recorders</a>, playing his <a href="/wiki/Rickenbacker_360/12" title="Rickenbacker 360/12">Rickenbacker 360/12</a> with session guitarist <a href="/wiki/Bill_Pitman" title="Bill Pitman">Bill Pitman</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Brian produced several backing tracks over a period lasting several months, using professional Hollywood recording studios and an ensemble that included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classically_trained" title="Classically trained">classically trained</a> <a href="/wiki/Session_musician" title="Session musician">session musicians</a> nicknamed "<a href="/wiki/The_Wrecking_Crew_(music)" title="The Wrecking Crew (music)">the Wrecking Crew</a>", also known as the musicians frequently employed on Phil Spector's records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoorefield201016_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoorefield201016-160">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114-50">[42]</a></sup> Of Spector's Wall of Sound technique, Wilson identified the <a href="/wiki/Tack_piano" title="Tack piano">tack piano</a> and organ mix in "I Know There's an Answer" as one example of himself applying the method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoorefield201016_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoorefield201016-160">[140]</a></sup> Wilson also doubled the bass (typically using an acoustic upright bass and an electric bass), guitars and keyboard parts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britz1997_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britz1997-161">[141]</a></sup> blending them with reverberation and adding other unusual instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (December 2014)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> <i>Rolling Stone</i> noted that the percussion in the album included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleigh_bells" title="Sleigh bells">sleigh bells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timpani" title="Timpani">timpani</a>, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCiro" title="Güiro">güiro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vibraphone" title="Vibraphone">vibraphone</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bongos" title="Bongos">bongos</a>, previously unused in rock music. The session musicians used Coke cans, water bottles and orange juice jugs for percussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Surviving tapes of his recording sessions show that he was open to his musicians, often taking advice and suggestions from them and even incorporating apparent mistakes if they provided a useful or interesting alternative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007114-50">[42]</a></sup> Brian said that he "was sort of a square" with the Wrecking Crew, starting his creative process with how each instrument sounded one-by-one, moving from keyboards, drums, then violins if they were not overdubbed. Although the self-taught Wilson often had entire arrangements worked out in his head, they were usually written in a shorthand form for the other players by one of his session musicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[nb 21]</a></sup> On notation and arranging, Brian clarified: "Sometimes I'd just write out a chord sheet and that would be for piano, organ, or harpsichord or anything. ... I wrote out all the horn charts separate from the keyboards. I wrote one basic keyboard chart, violins, horns, and basses, and percussion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-7"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Jardine, each member was taught their individual vocal lines by Brian at a piano. He explains, "Every night we'd come in for a playback. We'd sit around and listen to what we did the night before. Someone might say, well, that's pretty good but we can do that better ... We had somewhat photographic memory as far as the vocal parts were concerned so that never a problem for us."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharp2013_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharp2013-166">[145]</a></sup> This process proved to be the most exacting work the group had undertaken yet. During recording, Mike Love often called Brian "<a href="/wiki/Hearing_range#In_terrestrial_animals" title="Hearing range">dog ears</a>", a nickname referencing a canine's ability to detect sounds far beyond the limits of human hearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Love1997_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Love1997-167">[146]</a></sup> Love later summarized:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We worked and worked on the harmonies and, if there was the slightest little hint of a sharp or a flat, it wouldn't go on. We would do it over again until it was right. [Brian] was going for every subtle nuance that you could conceivably think of. Every voice had to be right, every voice and its resonance and tonality had to be right. The timing had to be right. The timbre of the voices just had to be correct, according to how he felt. And then he might, the next day, completely throw that out and we might have to do it over again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The majority of sessions were conducted at Western Studio 3 of <a href="/wiki/United_Western_Recorders" title="United Western Recorders">United Western Recorders</a>, with few additional tracking dates at <a href="/wiki/Gold_Star_Studios" title="Gold Star Studios">Gold Star Studios</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Sound_Recorders" title="Sunset Sound Recorders">Sunset Sound Recorders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromoff1996_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stromoff1996-168">[147]</a></sup> Some of the vocals were recorded at <a href="/wiki/CBS_Columbia_Square" title="CBS Columbia Square">CBS Columbia Square</a>, because it was the only facility in Los Angeles with an 8-track recorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromoff1996_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stromoff1996-168">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup> The album's final vocal overdubbing session took place on April 13, 1966, concluding a ten-month-long recording period that begun with "Sloop John B" in July 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200666.2C_84_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200666.2C_84-170">[149]</a></sup> The album was mixed three days later in a single nine-hour session.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200920_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200920-70">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[nb 22]</a></sup> Saxophonist <a href="/wiki/Steve_Douglas_(saxophonist)" title="Steve Douglas (saxophonist)">Steve Douglas</a> recalls of the album's draft mix: "It was full of noise. You could hear him talking in the background. It was real sloppy. He had spent all this time making the album, and zip – dubbed it down in one day or something like that. [When we said something to him about it], he took it back and mixed it properly. I think a lot of times, beautiful orchestrated stuff or parts got lost in his mixes"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Douglas1997_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Douglas1997-173">[151]</a></sup> Its total cost of production amounted to a then-unheard of $70,000 (equivalent to $520,000 in 2016).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaines2009146_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaines2009146-140">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>A true stereophonic mix of <i>Pet Sounds</i> was not considered in 1966 largely because of mixing logistics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup> In spite of whether a true stereo mix was possible, Brian intentionally mixed the final version of his recordings in mono (as did Spector). He did this because he felt that mono mastering provided more sonic control over the final result, irrespective of the vagaries of speaker placement and sound system quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[nb 23]</a></sup> Another and more personal reason for Brian's preference for mono was his almost total deafness in his right ear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007-175">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[nb 24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering">Engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">

<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">A <a href="/wiki/Leslie_speaker" title="Leslie speaker">Leslie speaker</a> was used to filter lead guitar for the title track "<a href="/wiki/Pet_Sounds_(instrumental)" title="Pet Sounds (instrumental)">Pet Sounds</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waspensky1997_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waspensky1997-177">[153]</a></sup> Other quirks from this recording include <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> cans and a <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCiro" title="Güiro">güiro</a> as percussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tracks_36-8"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-36">[30]</a></sup></div>

<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>

</table>
<p>Although Spector's trademark sound was aurally complex, many of the best-known Wall of Sound recordings were recorded on <a href="/wiki/Ampex" title="Ampex">Ampex</a> 3-track recorders. Spector's backing tracks were recorded live, and usually in a single take. These backing tracks were mixed live, in mono, and taped directly onto one track of the 3-track recorder;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buskin2007_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buskin2007-178">[154]</a></sup> <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">instrumental overdubs only rarely added.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The lead vocal was then taped, usually (though not always) as an uninterrupted live performance, recorded direct to the second track of the recorder. The master was completed with the addition of backing vocals on the third track before the three tracks were mixed down to create the mono master tape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buskin2007_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buskin2007-178">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>By comparison, Brian produced tracks that were of greater technical complexity by using state-of-the-art 4-track and 8-track recorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-4"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup> Most backing tracks were recorded onto a 4-track recorder before being later dubbed down (in mono) onto one track of an 8-track machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoorefield201018_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoorefield201018-179">[155]</a></sup> Brian typically divided instruments by three tracks: drums–percussion–keyboard, horns, and bass–additional percussion–guitar. The fourth track usually contained a rough reference mix used during playback at the session,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="How exactly was this mix used as a reference during playback or after? (January 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> later to be erased for overdubs such as a string section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-5"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup> After mixing down the 4-track to mono for overdubbing via an 8-track recorder, six of the remaining seven tracks were usually dedicated to each of the Beach Boys' vocals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-6"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup> The last track was usually reserved for additional elements such as extra vocals or instrumentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tracks_36-9"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-36">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[nb 25]</a></sup> Vocals were recorded using two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neumann_U-47" title="Neumann U-47">Neumann U-47s</a>, which Dennis, Carl and Jardine would sing on, and a Shure 545 used by Brian for his leads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britz1997_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britz1997-161">[141]</a></sup> Love sang most of the album's bass vocals, and necessitated an extra microphone due to his low volume range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Love1997_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Love1997-167">[146]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scully_280_4-track_tape_recorder,_Ardent_Studios_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="3000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Scully_280_4-track_tape_recorder%2C_Ardent_Studios_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Scully_280_4-track_tape_recorder%2C_Ardent_Studios_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Scully_280_4-track_tape_recorder%2C_Ardent_Studios_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Scully_280_4-track_tape_recorder%2C_Ardent_Studios_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Scully_280_4-track_tape_recorder%2C_Ardent_Studios_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Scully_280_4-track_tape_recorder%2C_Ardent_Studios_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scully_280_4-track_tape_recorder,_Ardent_Studios_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Scully 4-track 280 tape deck, similar to the 288 used for <i>Pet Sounds</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Pet Sounds</i> was recorded on Scully 4-track 288 tape decks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromoff1996_168-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stromoff1996-168">[147]</a></sup> The only tape effects used on the album are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slapback_echo" title="Slapback echo">slapback echo</a> and reverb. Sound engineer <a href="/wiki/Mark_Linett" title="Mark Linett">Mark Linett</a> notes: "to my ears, it sounds more like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plate_reverb" title="Plate reverb">plate [reverberators]</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Echo_chamber" title="Echo chamber">chambers</a>. It should be mentioned that you get a significantly different sound from a chamber when you record it ‘live’ as opposed to doing it off tape, and one reason these records sound the way they do is that the reverb was being printed as part of the recording – unlike today where we’ll record ‘dry’ and add the effects later."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromoff1996_168-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stromoff1996-168">[147]</a></sup> By the time of <i>Pet Sounds</i>, Brian was using up to six of the eight tracks on the multitrack master so that he could record the voice of each member separately, allowing him greater control over the vocal balance in the final mix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-8"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unreleased_material">Unreleased material</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Unreleased material">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Good_Vibrations" title="Good Vibrations">Good Vibrations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trombone_Dixie" title="Trombone Dixie">Trombone Dixie</a></div>
<p>On October 15, 1965, Brian went to the studio with a 43-piece orchestra to record an instrumental piece entitled "Three Blind Mice", which bore no musical connection to the <a href="/wiki/Nursery_rhyme" title="Nursery rhyme">nursery rhyme</a> <a href="/wiki/Three_Blind_Mice" title="Three Blind Mice">of the same name</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runtagh2016_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runtagh2016-181">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[nb 26]</a></sup> Another instrumental, called "<a href="/wiki/Trombone_Dixie" title="Trombone Dixie">Trombone Dixie</a>", was recorded. According to Brian, "I was just foolin' around one day, fuckin' around with the musicians, and I took that arrangement out of my briefcase and we did it in 20 minutes. It was nothing, there was really nothing in it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[nb 27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid February 1966, Brian was in the studio with his session band taping the first takes of a new composition, "<a href="/wiki/Good_Vibrations" title="Good Vibrations">Good Vibrations</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup> On February 23, he gave Capitol a provisional track listing for the new LP, which included both "Sloop John B" and "Good Vibrations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-3"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup> This contradicts the long-held misconception that "Sloop John B" was a forced inclusion as the hit single at Capitol's insistence: in late February, the song was weeks away from release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-4"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuckley20037_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuckley20037-185">[158]</a></sup> Brian worked through February and into March fine-tuning the backing tracks. To the group's surprise, he also dropped "Good Vibrations" from the running order, telling them that he wanted to spend more time on it. Al Jardine remembered: "At the time, we all had assumed that 'Good Vibrations' was going to be on the album, but Brian decided to hold it out. It was a judgment call on his part; we felt otherwise but left the ultimate decision up to him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-5"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brian devoted some <i>Pet Sounds</i> sessions to avant-garde indulgences such as an extended <i>a cappella</i> run-through of the <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_song" title="Children's song">children's song</a> "<a href="/wiki/Row,_Row,_Row_Your_Boat" title="Row, Row, Row Your Boat">Row, Row, Row Your Boat</a>" that exploited the song's use of <a href="/wiki/Round_(music)" title="Round (music)">rounds</a>. Humorous skits and sound effects were recorded in an attempt to create a psychedelic <a href="/wiki/Comedy_album" title="Comedy album">comedy album</a>, foreshadowing much of his work on <i><a href="/wiki/Smile_(The_Beach_Boys_album)" title="Smile (The Beach Boys album)">Smile</a></i>, which was set to have followed <i>Pet Sounds</i>. The only product of these sessions present in <i>Pet Sounds</i> was an excerpt of Brian's dogs barking accompanied by a recording of passing trains, which was <a href="/wiki/Sampling_(music)" title="Sampling (music)">sampled</a> from the 1963 sound effects LP <i>Mister D's Machine</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runtagh2016_181-2"><a href="#cite_note-Runtagh2016-181">[156]</a></sup> Brian may also have briefly considered recording other animal sounds for inclusion, as evidenced by a snippet of surviving studio chatter from a "dog barking" session. This features Brian asking studio engineer <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Britz" title="Chuck Britz">Chuck Britz</a>: "Hey, Chuck, is it possible we can bring a horse in here without ... if we don't screw everything up?", to which a clearly startled Britz responds, "I <i>beg</i> your pardon?", with Brian then pleading, "Honest to God, now, the horse is tame and everything!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez201483_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez201483-186">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_and_cover_art">Title and cover art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Title and cover art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brian%2BWilson%2BPet%2BSounds%2BEra%2BBrian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="500" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/31/Brian%2BWilson%2BPet%2BSounds%2BEra%2BBrian.jpg/175px-Brian%2BWilson%2BPet%2BSounds%2BEra%2BBrian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/31/Brian%2BWilson%2BPet%2BSounds%2BEra%2BBrian.jpg/263px-Brian%2BWilson%2BPet%2BSounds%2BEra%2BBrian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/31/Brian%2BWilson%2BPet%2BSounds%2BEra%2BBrian.jpg/350px-Brian%2BWilson%2BPet%2BSounds%2BEra%2BBrian.jpg 2x" width="175"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brian%2BWilson%2BPet%2BSounds%2BEra%2BBrian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brian Wilson posing with a goat at the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Zoo" title="San Diego Zoo">San Diego Zoo</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The front sleeve depicts a snapshot of the band — from left, they are Carl, Brian, and Dennis Wilson; Mike Love; and Al Jardine — feeding pieces of apples to seven goats at the zoo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goats2016_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goats2016-187">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[nb 28]</a></sup> The photo was taken on February 10, 1966, when the group traveled to the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Zoo" title="San Diego Zoo">San Diego Zoo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIGS_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-GIGS-21">[20]</a></sup> accompanied by the photographer George Jerman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman2004116_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman2004116-190">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Love remembers that Capitol's working title for the album was <i>Our Freaky Friends</i>, and that the label planned the cover shoot at the zoo, with the animals representing the group's "freaky friends".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELove2016133_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELove2016133-191">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[nb 29]</a></sup> In May, an article published by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Diego_Union" title="San Diego Union">San Diego Union</a></i> wrote that the group "came down from Hollywood to take a cover picture for their forthcoming album <i>Our Freaky Friends</i>. ... Zoo officials were not keen about having their beloved beasts connected with the title of the album, but gave in when the Beach Boys explained that animals are an 'in' thing with teenagers. And that the Beach Boys were rushing to beat the rock and roll group called <a href="/wiki/The_Animals" title="The Animals">The Animals</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[nb 30]</a></sup> Asher remembered: "[Brian] had some proofs of the pictures they'd done at the zoo, and he told me they were thinking of calling the album <i>Pet Sounds</i>. I thought it was a goofy name for an album — I thought it trivialized what we had accomplished."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranata200382_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranata200382-196">[166]</a></sup> Until arriving to the photo shoot, Jardine thought that "pet" referred to slang for <a href="/wiki/Making_out" title="Making out">making out</a> ("petting"). Jardine also expressed disappointment with the chosen cover, believing it was "crazy" to go to the zoo, that "the art department screwed up pretty badly on that one ... [I] wanted a more sensitive and enlightening cover."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name <i>Pet Sounds</i> was Love's invention, according to himself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-7"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup> Brian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goats2016_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goats2016-187">[160]</a></sup> and Jardine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goats2016_187-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goats2016-187">[160]</a></sup> Brian explained that the album was named "after the dogs ... That was the whole idea",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPreiss197954_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPreiss197954-198">[168]</a></sup> and that the title was a "tribute" to Spector by matching his initials (PS),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2007152_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2007152-199">[169]</a></sup> but could not recall who thought of going to the zoo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goats2016_187-3"><a href="#cite_note-Goats2016-187">[160]</a></sup> Love recounted: "We were standing in the hallway in one of the recording studios, either Western or Columbia, and we didn't have a title. ... We had taken pictures at the zoo and ... there were animal sounds on the record, and we were thinking, well, it's our favorite music of that time, so I said, 'Why don't we call it <i>Pet Sounds</i>?'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-8"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Love1997_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-Love1997-167">[146]</a></sup> Brian has also credited Carl for the title,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SongBySong_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-SongBySong-66">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-9"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup> while Carl said with uncertainty that it might have come from Brian: "The idea he [Brian] had was that everybody has these sounds that they love, and this was a collection of his 'pet sounds.' It was hard to think of a name for the album, because you sure couldn't call it <i><a href="/wiki/Shut_Down_Volume_2" title="Shut Down Volume 2">Shut Down Vol. 3</a></i> .. It was just so much more than a record; it had such a spiritual quality. It wasn't going in and doing another top ten. It had so much more meaning than that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carl1997_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carl1997-88">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[nb 31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Personally, I think the group has evolved another 800 per cent in the last year. We have a more conscious, arty production now that’s more polished. It’s all been like an explosion for us. ... it's like I'm in the golden age of what it's all about.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">—Brian Wilson to <i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">Melody Maker</a></i>, March 1966<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MelodyMakerBlast_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-MelodyMakerBlast-65">[53]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>After the album was assembled, Brian brought a complete acetate to Marilyn, who remembers, "It was so beautiful, one of the most spiritual times of my whole life. We both cried. Right after we listened to it, he said he was scared that nobody was going to like it. That it was too intricate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marilyn1997_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marilyn1997-79">[65]</a></sup> <i>Pet Sounds</i> was released on May 16, debuting on the <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> charts at 106.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BillboardMay1966_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-BillboardMay1966-203">[172]</a></sup> Compared to their previous albums in the US, <i>Pet Sounds</i> garnered dramatically less commercial success, and peaked at No. 10 on the <i>Billboard</i> album chart. US sales numbered approximately 500,000 units, a significant drop-off from the chain of million-selling albums which immediately preceded it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200685_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200685-204">[173]</a></sup> <i>Pet Sounds</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span> initial release was not awarded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gold_album" title="Gold album">gold</a> certification by the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a> (RIAA). Eventually, <i>Pet Sounds</i> was presented with gold and platinum awards in 2000 based on sales that could be documented, although Capitol Records estimated it may have sold over two million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riaagold_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-riaagold-205">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brian was "heartsick" that <i>Pet Sounds</i> didn't sell as highly as he expected, and interpreted the response as a rejection to his creative ideals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-8"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup> Some blamed Capitol Records for the album's underwhelming sales. Allegedly, they did not promote the album as heavily as previous releases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carl1997_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carl1997-88">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston1997_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston1997-207">[176]</a></sup> Carl stated that Capitol did not feel a need to promote the Beach Boys since they were getting so much airplay, and that they had a "set image" for the group which even <i>Pet Sounds</i> could not alter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carl1997_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-Carl1997-88">[72]</a></sup> Others assumed that the label considered the album a risk, appealing more to an older demographic than the younger, female audience the Beach Boys built their commercial standing on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones200847_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones200847-208">[177]</a></sup> To Brian's dismay, within two months, Capitol assembled the group's first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greatest_hits" title="Greatest hits">greatest hits</a> compilation, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Best_of_The_Beach_Boys" title="Best of The Beach Boys">Best of The Beach Boys</a></i>, which was quickly certified gold by the RIAA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200685.E2.80.9386_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200685.E2.80.9386-209">[178]</a></sup> Capitol executive <a href="/wiki/Karl_Engemann" title="Karl Engemann">Karl Engemann</a> later speculated: "This is just conjecture on my part because it was so long ago ... because the marketing people didn't believe that <i>Pet Sounds</i> was going to do that well, they were probably looking for some additional volume in that quarter. There's a good possibility that's what happened. Anyway, my real forte was dealing with artists and producers and making them feel comfortable so they could achieve their ends. And sometimes, particularly when the label wanted something that the artist didn't, it wasn't easy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before <i>Pet Sounds</i> was released, "Caroline, No" was issued as a single; it was credited to Brian alone, leading to speculation that he was considering leaving the band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKent200923_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKent200923-211">[180]</a></sup> The single reached number 32 in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardsingles_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-billboardsingles-212">[181]</a></sup> It was followed by "Sloop John B", credited to the Beach Boys. The single reached number 3 in the US<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardsingles_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-billboardsingles-212">[181]</a></sup> and number 2 in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-everyhit_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-everyhit-213">[182]</a></sup> It had a four-weeks stay at number 1 in the Netherlands, making it the "Hit of the Year".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[183]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (February 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Two months after the album's release, "Wouldn't It Be Nice" was released, later reaching number 8 in the US, where it was treated as the A-side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardsingles_212-2"><a href="#cite_note-billboardsingles-212">[181]</a></sup> Its flip side, "God Only Knows," was featured as the A-side in Europe, peaking at number 2 in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-everyhit_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-everyhit-213">[182]</a></sup> As a B-side in the US, it reached number 39.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardsingles_212-3"><a href="#cite_note-billboardsingles-212">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom_EMI_release">United Kingdom EMI release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: United Kingdom EMI release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The album's greatest success was in the UK, where it reached number 2<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-everyhit_213-2"><a href="#cite_note-everyhit-213">[182]</a></sup> and stayed in the top-ten positions for six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGillett1984329_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGillett1984329-215">[184]</a></sup> It was aided by support from the British music industry, who embraced the record. Bruce Johnston stated that while he flew to London in May 1966, a number of musicians and other guests gathered in his hotel suite to listen to repeated playbacks of the album. This included <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keith_Moon" title="Keith Moon">Keith Moon</a>. Moon himself involved Johnston by helping him gain coverage in British television circuits, and connecting him with Lennon and McCartney. Johnston claimed that <i>Pet Sounds</i> got so much publicity, "it forced <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI</a> to put the album out sooner."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston1997_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston1997-207">[176]</a></sup> The Beach Boys became the strongest selling album act in the UK for the final quarter of 1966, dethroning the three-year reign of native bands such as the Beatles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to author Steven Gaines, <a href="/wiki/Derek_Taylor" title="Derek Taylor">Derek Taylor</a>, the Beach Boys' press officer, is widely recognized as having been instrumental in this success, due to his longstanding connections with the Beatles and other industry figures in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaines1995152_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaines1995152-217">[186]</a></sup> The music press there carried advertisements saying that <i>Pet Sounds</i> was "The Most Progressive Pop Album Ever!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pc37_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-pc37-59">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez201481_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez201481-101">[85]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> founding editor <a href="/wiki/Jann_Wenner" title="Jann Wenner">Jann Wenner</a> later recalled that fans in the UK identified the Beach Boys as being "years ahead" of the Beatles and declared Wilson a "genius"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pc37_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-pc37-59">[49]</a></sup> – the latter being a description of the group's leader that Taylor had helped to establish among local music journalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaines1995152.2C_169_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaines1995152.2C_169-218">[187]</a></sup> Although it has been claimed that <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">the Rolling Stones</a>' manager <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrew_Oldham" title="Andrew Oldham">Andrew Oldham</a> helped Taylor publicize unsolicited advertisements lauding the album in British music papers, a search of the UK pop press for 1966 fails to uncover any such advertisement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFusilli2005111_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFusilli2005111-219">[188]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (April 2016)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Two music videos were filmed set to "Sloop John B" and "God Only Knows" for the UK's <i><a href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops" title="Top of the Pops">Top of the Pops</a></i>, both directed by Taylor. The first was filmed at Brian's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laurel_Canyon" title="Laurel Canyon">Laurel Way</a> home with Dennis acting as cameraman, the second near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Arrowhead,_California" title="Lake Arrowhead, California">Lake Arrowhead</a>. While the second film, containing footage of the group minus Bruce flailing around in grotesque horror masks and playing <a href="/wiki/Old_maid_(card_game)" title="Old maid (card game)">Old Maid</a>, was intended to be accompanied by excerpts from "Wouldn't It Be Nice," "Here Today," and "God Only Knows," slight edits were made by the BBC to reduce the film's length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman2004130.E2.80.93131_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman2004130.E2.80.93131-220">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Early reviews for the album in the US ranged from negative to tentatively positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200685_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200685-204">[173]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s reviewer called it an "exciting, well-produced LP" with "two superb instrumental cuts", and highlighted the "strong single potential" of "Wouldn't It Be Nice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BillboardMay1966_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-BillboardMay1966-203">[172]</a></sup> By contrast, the reception from music journalists in the UK was highly favourable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranata2003201.E2.80.93202_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranata2003201.E2.80.93202-221">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeaf198576.2C_87.E2.80.9388_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeaf198576.2C_87.E2.80.9388-222">[191]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Record_Mirror" title="Record Mirror">Record Mirror</a></i> reported that the LP had been "widely praised".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[192]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Penny_Valentine" title="Penny Valentine">Penny Valentine</a> of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disc_and_Music_Echo" title="Disc and Music Echo">Disc and Music Echo</a></i> admired it as "Thirteen tracks of Brian Wilson genius … The whole LP is far more romantic than the usual Beach Boys jollity: sad little wistful songs about lost love and found love and all-around love."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranata2003202_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranata2003202-224">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">Melody Maker</a></i> ran a feature which interviewed many pop musicians on whether they believed that the album was truly revolutionary or progressive. The author concluded that "the record's impact on artists and the men behind the artists has been considerable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman2004139_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman2004139-225">[194]</a></sup> Andrew Oldham said: "I think that <i>Pet Sounds</i> is the most progressive album of the year in as much as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rimsky-Korsakov" title="Rimsky-Korsakov">Rimsky-Korsakov</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Scheherazade_(Rimsky-Korsakov)" title="Scheherazade (Rimsky-Korsakov)">Scheherazade</a></i> was. It's the pop equivalent of that, a complete exercise in pop music."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman2004139_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman2004139-225">[194]</a></sup> In Gene Sculatti's 1968 editorial "In Defense of the Beach Boys", for <i>Jazz &amp; Pop</i>, he commented that Wilson was "one of the all-time great composers of melody in rock" along with <a href="/wiki/Lennon%E2%80%93McCartney" title="Lennon–McCartney">Lennon–McCartney</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Phillips_(musician)" title="John Phillips (musician)">John Phillips</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Smokey_Robinson" title="Smokey Robinson">Smokey Robinson</a>, yet, "<i>Pet Sounds</i> was by no means a revolutionary work in that it inspired or influenced the rock scene in a big way. It was revolutionary only within the confines of the Beach Boys' music." However, later in the piece he conceded: "<i>Pet Sounds</i> was a final statement of an era and a prophecy that sweeping changes lay ahead."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retrospective_reviews">Retrospective reviews</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Retrospective reviews">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle"><i><a href="/wiki/Blender_(magazine)" title="Blender (magazine)">Blender</a></i></td>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle"><span style="white-space:nowrap" title="5/5 stars"><img alt="5/5 stars" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="108" height="11"
