<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pet_Sounds&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-3" style="-moz-column-count: 3; -webkit-column-count: 3; column-count: 3; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">During the spring of 1965, Brian had what he considered to be "a very religious experience" after consuming a full dose of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSD" title="LSD">LSD</a>. He stated, "I learned a lot of things, like patience, understanding. I can't teach you, or tell you what I learned from taking it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman2004136_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman2004136-15">[15]</a></sup> A week after his first LSD trip, he began suffering from <a href="/wiki/Auditory_hallucination" title="Auditory hallucination">auditory hallucinations</a>, which have persisted throughout his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ability_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ability-16">[16]</a></sup> Wilson also stated: "I wrote <i>Pet Sounds</i> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marijuana" title="Marijuana">marijuana</a> — not on it, but I utilized marijuana now and then for <i>Pet Sounds</i>. ... It gave me the ability — <i>carte blanche</i> — to create something, you know what I mean? And that’s where it’s at; drugs aren’t where it’s at. But, for me, that’s where it <i>was</i> at in 1966."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varga2016_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varga2016-17">[17]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<a href="/wiki/The_Little_Girl_I_Once_Knew" title="The Little Girl I Once Knew">The Little Girl I Once Knew</a>", "<a href="/wiki/You_Still_Believe_in_Me" title="You Still Believe in Me">In My Childhood</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Talk_(Put_Your_Head_on_My_Shoulder)" title="Don't Talk (Put Your Head on My Shoulder)">Don't Talk (Put Your Head on My Shoulder)</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Pet_Sounds_(instrumental)" title="Pet Sounds (instrumental)">Run, James, Run</a>", "Trombone Dixie", and "Three Blind Mice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIGS_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIGS-21">[20]</a></sup> "Run, James, Run" was the working title for the instrumental "<a href="/wiki/Pet_Sounds_(instrumental)" title="Pet Sounds (instrumental)">Pet Sounds</a>", the suggestion being that it would be offered for use in a <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a> movie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although it had lyrics, Wilson refused to show them to Asher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Loren Schwartz (now Lorren Daro) was an assistant at the <a href="/wiki/William_Morris_Agency" title="William Morris Agency">William Morris Agency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman200487_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman200487-28">[25]</a></sup> Daro introduced Wilson to LSD, recounting that his dosage was "one hundred and twenty-five mics of pure <a href="/wiki/Owsley_Stanley" title="Owsley Stanley">Owsley</a>," and that "he had the full-on <a href="/wiki/Ego_death" title="Ego death">ego death</a>. It was a beautiful thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin2006174.E2.80.93175_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin2006174.E2.80.93175-29">[26]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While it is often said that Brian composed all of the music to <i>Pet Sounds</i>, it has been claimed that Asher made significant musical contributions to "<a href="/wiki/I_Just_Wasn%27t_Made_for_These_Times" title="I Just Wasn't Made for These Times">I Just Wasn't Made for These Times</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Caroline,_No" title="Caroline, No">Caroline, No</a>", and "That's Not Me".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaines2009145_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaines2009145-31">[27]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In November 1965, Wilson recorded instrumental versions of the standards "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/How_Deep_Is_the_Ocean" title="How Deep Is the Ocean">How Deep Is the Ocean</a>" and "Stella by Starlight". According to Leaf, it was a coincidence that the latter turned out to be a favorite of Asher's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeafPerspective_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeafPerspective-34">[29]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The exact degree of Love's contribution to "Wouldn't It Be Nice" was never fully determined, but under oath in a court of law, Asher stated it consisted of the tag "<i>Good night my baby/Sleep tight, my baby</i>" and possible minor vocal arrangement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tracks_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-36">[30]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wilson recalls that it was Asher who introduced him to <i>Rubber Soul</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrianWilson1997_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-BrianWilson1997-22">[21]</a></sup> it being the alternate US version that was configured by Capitol Records to have a cohesive <a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">folk rock</a> sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RS_Guide_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-RS_Guide-46">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Johnston" title="Bruce Johnston">Bruce Johnston</a> remembers listening to the album at around Christmastime 1965 "with <a href="/wiki/Terry_Melcher" title="Terry Melcher">Terry Melcher</a> and his mother (<a href="/wiki/Doris_Day" title="Doris Day">Doris Day</a>) ... with us were Brian, Mike, and <a href="/wiki/John_Phillips_(musician)" title="John Phillips (musician)">John Phillips</a> (of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Mamas_and_The_Papas" title="The Mamas and The Papas">The Mamas and The Papas</a>). Brian said he thought that <i>Rubber Soul</i> was a great thematic pop album."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[40]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine describes the Capitol version as "a folk-rock album more conceptually unified than the U.K. original – though shorter, and not as good".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RS_Guide_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-RS_Guide-46">[39]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the US, no singles were released from the album, which Leaf says created the "perception that their new work should be viewed as a whole. ... it introduced the possibility to the recording industry that the 45 RPM disc...preeminent for a decade...might soon give way to a new king...the album as a work of art."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeafPerspective_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-LeafPerspective-34">[29]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wilson's previous habits, evident in <i>Today!</i> and <i>Summer Days,</i> were to sacrifice portions of an album with lesser, superficial material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580-49">[41]</a></sup> <i>Today!</i> also contained five songs with a unified theme located on the album's second side, similar to what Wilson would endeavor for the whole of <i>Pet Sounds</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guriel2016_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guriel2016-52">[43]</a></sup> Author Scott Schinder referred to <i>Today!</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> "<a href="/wiki/Suite_(music)" title="Suite (music)">suite-like structure</a>" as an early example of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Album_Era" title="Album Era">rock album format</a> being used to make a cohesive artistic statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007111_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007111-53">[44]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wilson has said that the main difference between him and the Beatles is that the Beatles "will simplify to its skeletal form an arrangement," whereas Wilson would be "impelled to make it more complex," and that if he had arranged "<a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Wood_(This_Bird_Has_Flown)" title="Norwegian Wood (This Bird Has Flown)">Norwegian Wood</a>", he would have "orchestrated it, put in background voices, [and] done a thousand things".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFusilli200580-49">[41]</a></sup> Musicologist Philip Lambert called the Beatles' general sound and style "vastly different" from <i>Pet Sounds</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELambert2007225_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELambert2007225-58">[48]</a></sup> In 1969, Love denied that they musically influenced Wilson: "He was in his own little world. If he were influenced by the Beatles, there'd be more <a href="/wiki/Fuzz_bass" title="Fuzz bass">fuzz tone</a> and a few <a href="/wiki/Sitar" title="Sitar">sitars</a> on our records, but there never have been, really."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pc37_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-pc37-59">[49]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mark Dillon believes that the album "channels" <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Riddle" title="Nelson Riddle">Nelson Riddle</a> more than it does Spector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon2012152_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon2012152-62">[51]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other artists who influenced specific content on the album included <a href="/wiki/Burt_Bacharach" title="Burt Bacharach">Burt Bacharach</a> (on "Let's Go Away for Awhile"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SongBySong_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-SongBySong-66">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Kingston_Trio" title="The Kingston Trio">The Kingston Trio</a> (on "Sloop John B"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jardine1997_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jardine1997-19">[18]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Miller" title="Glenn Miller">Glenn Miller</a> (on "Caroline, No").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SongBySong_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-SongBySong-66">[54]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WBUR" title="WBUR">WBUR</a>'s Seth Rogovoy wrote that "Wilson upends and overturns every Beach Boys cliché, exposing the hollowness at their core. ... Whereas up until this point the group had celebrated the endless summer of adolescence as the bards of fun and teenage romance, Wilson starts right out with a 180-degree turn — “Wouldn’t it be nice if we were older.” What? Really? ... The songs pile up in this vein."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WBUR2016_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-WBUR2016-72">[59]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Love was reported to have found Asher's lyrics "nauseating", declaring the album "Brian's ego music", and urging him not to "fuck with the formula".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlin200684_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlin200684-83">[69]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1999, Wilson explained: "It was an inappropriate lyric. ... I just thought that to say 'Hang on to your ego' was an ego statement in and of itself, which I wasn't going for, so I changed it. I gave it a lot of thought."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bittersweet1999_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bittersweet1999-85">[70]</a></sup> Love said: "I was aware that Brian was beginning to experiment with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSD" title="LSD">LSD</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychedelic_drugs" title="Psychedelic drugs">psychedelics</a> ... The prevailing drug jargon at the time had it that doses of LSD would shatter your <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ego_(Freudian)" title="Ego (Freudian)">ego</a>, as if that were a positive thing ... I wasn't interested in taking acid or getting rid of my ego."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tracks_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-36">[30]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-114">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Others labelled the album:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Experimental_pop" title="Experimental pop">Experimental pop</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_West_Australian" title="The West Australian">The West Australian</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins2016_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Collins2016-78">[64]</a></sup></span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symphonic_rock" title="Symphonic rock">Symphonic rock</a> (<a href="/wiki/Domenic_Priore" title="Domenic Priore">Domenic Priore</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriore2005_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriore2005-110">[94]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chamber_pop" title="Chamber pop">Chamber pop</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">Folk rock</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tim_Sommer" title="Tim Sommer">Tim Sommer</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sommer2015_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sommer2015-112">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sommer2016_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sommer2016-113">[97]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-138"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-138">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Marilyn believed that her relationship with Brian was a central reference within the album's lyrics; namely on "You Still Believe in Me" and "Caroline, No".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFusilli200584_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFusilli200584-137">[120]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On Wilson's creative process, Asher remembers that "one of the things that I really enjoyed, was that first time I'd hear him hunting for a chord change and I'd think 'Man, he's just gone right off the edge because he's not ever gonna get close. He's way out there in some area that's just—he'll never get back. And if he's successful, gets out of there, people are going to say, I've lost my <a href="/wiki/Tonic_(music)" title="Tonic (music)">tone center</a>, don't know where the hell I am and stuff. And then, eventually, he'd figure out what it was he wanted to do. A lot of it was just hunting and pecking, the way some of us type."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tony_Asher_interview_25-11"><a href="#cite_note-Tony_Asher_interview-25">[23]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Throughout the latter half of the 1960s, Wilson was repeatedly shown to have become interested in Eastern philosophy and the psychedelic experience, often pointing to <a href="/wiki/Ego_death" title="Ego death">ego loss</a> as the key to a better way of living.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Out-Of-Sight.21_SMiLE_Timeline_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Out-Of-Sight.21_SMiLE_Timeline-147">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoestler198938_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoestler198938-148">[129]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (May 2014)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Jim DeRogatis surmised that Wilson's LSD use led him to write more introspective work, a contrast from the Beatles, whom after taking LSD began addressing problems in the world around them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis200334_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis200334-135">[118]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-165"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-165">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For his session of "<a href="/wiki/I_Just_Wasn%27t_Made_for_These_Times" title="I Just Wasn't Made for These Times">I Just Wasn't Made for These Times</a>", <a href="/wiki/Paul_Tanner" title="Paul Tanner">Paul Tanner</a> remembered: "Brian came over to me and sang such and such a thing, and I said 'Well, write it down and I'll play it,' and he said 'Write it down? We don't write anything down—if you want it written down you have to write it down yourself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrend200518_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrend200518-164">[144]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-172"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-172">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1995, it emerged that this session was originally intended to add vocals to "Let's Go Away for Awhile", but Capitol insisted that the session date be used for the album's mixing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200924_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoeTobler200924-171">[150]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-174"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-174">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In that era, radio and TV were broadcast in mono and most domestic and automotive radios and record players were monophonic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-3"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-176"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-176">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Brian's deafness is rumored to be the result of childhood injury to his <a href="/wiki/Eardrum" title="Eardrum">eardrum</a> caused by a blow from his violent father <a href="/wiki/Murry_Wilson" title="Murry Wilson">Murry Wilson</a>, although Brian claimed that he was born deaf in one ear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchinder2007_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchinder2007-175">[152]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-180"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-180">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The survival of most of these 4-track instrumental masters and 8-track vocal masters, permitted Capitol to create the high-fidelity stereo remixes of the isolated backing tracks and vocal tracks which appear on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pet_Sounds_Sessions" title="The Pet Sounds Sessions">The Pet Sounds Sessions</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linett1997_169-7"><a href="#cite_note-Linett1997-169">[148]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-182"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-182">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was included as part of the Beach Boys' 2011 release of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Smile_Sessions" title="The Smile Sessions">The Smile Sessions</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runtagh2016_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Runtagh2016-181">[156]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-184"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-184">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It remained in the vaults until its inclusion on the album's 1990 remastered CD release.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-189"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-189">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bruce Johnston, who joined the band one year earlier, does not appear on the cover due to contractual restraints from <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia Records</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman2004116.E2.80.93117_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman2004116.E2.80.93117-188">[161]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-192"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-192">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to historian Brad Elliot, <i>Pet Sounds</i> was chosen as the album's title before its cover photo was taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotLinerNotes1999_162-6"><a href="#cite_note-ElliotLinerNotes1999-162">[142]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-195"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-195">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The article continues to say that the group mishandled and discomforted many of the animals: "Before they left, said zoo public relations director Bill Seaton, the caged animals were 'about to crack up.' ... The zoo superintendent John Muth, an ex marine sergeant major, said the Beach Boys are not welcome back and never will be."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[164]</a></sup> Johnston referenced this, saying "The goats were horrible! ... The zoo said we were torturing the animals but they should have seen what we had to go through. We were doing all the suffering."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadman2004117_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadman2004117-194">[165]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Brian's discredited 1991 autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/Wouldn%27t_It_Be_Nice:_My_Own_Story" title="Wouldn't It Be Nice: My Own Story">Wouldn't It Be Nice: My Own Story</a></i>, it was written that the title was inspired by Mike Love asking "Who's gonna hear this shit? The ears of a dog?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison199754_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison199754-200">[170]</a></sup> In 2016, Love denied that he had ever said the comment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BalladofML_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-BalladofML-201">[171]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-261"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-261">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Vernon Joyson omitted the Beach Boys and <i>Pet Sounds</i> from his book <i>The Acid Trip: A Complete Guide to Psychedelic Music</i> on the basis that they "essentially predated the <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_era" title="Psychedelic era">psychedelic era</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJoyson19848_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJoyson19848-156">[136]</a></sup> Music journalist <a href="/wiki/Jim_DeRogatis" title="Jim DeRogatis">Jim DeRogatis</a> said that it was one of the first psychedelic rock masterpieces, along with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Psychedelic_Sounds_of_the_13th_Floor_Elevators" title="The Psychedelic Sounds of the 13th Floor Elevators">The Psychedelic Sounds of the 13th Floor Elevators</a></i> (1966) and <i>Revolver</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis2003xi_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeRogatis2003xi-96">[80]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-268"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-268">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Regarding the influence of <i>Pet Sounds</i> on early 2000s emo music, writer Sean Cureton believes that <a href="/wiki/Weezer" title="Weezer">Weezer</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Pinkerton_(album)" title="Pinkerton (album)">Pinkerton</a></i> (1996) and <a href="/wiki/Death_Cab_for_Cutie" title="Death Cab for Cutie">Death Cab for Cutie</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Transatlanticism" title="Transatlanticism">Transatlanticism</a></i> (2003) "immediately springing to mind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cureton_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cureton-267">[235]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-271"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-271">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Walter_Everett_(musicologist)" title="Walter Everett (musicologist)">Walter Everett</a>, citing a quantitative study of <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">tempos</a> in music from the era, identifies the Beatles' <i>Rubber Soul</i> as a work that was "made more to be thought about than danced to", and an album that "began a far-reaching trend" in its slowing-down of the tempos typically used in pop and rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett2001311.E2.80.93312_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett2001311.E2.80.93312-270">[237]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-274"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-274">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 2010, <i>Pet Sounds</i> was listed in <a href="/wiki/Classic_Rock_(magazine)" title="Classic Rock (magazine)"><i>Classic Rock</i></a>'s "50 Albums That Built Prog Rock".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProgRockRoots_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-ProgRockRoots-273">[239]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-286"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-286">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Townshend later stated: "'God Only Knows' is simple and elegant and was stunning when it first appeared; it still sounds perfect".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[250]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-296"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-296">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Pet Sounds</i> also influenced McCartney's composition "<a href="/wiki/Here,_There_and_Everywhere" title="Here, There and Everywhere">Here, There and Everywhere</a>", from <i>Revolver</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[259]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-313"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-313">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_cappela" title="A cappela">a cappela</a> demo section of "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Talk_(Put_Your_Head_on_My_Shoulder)" title="Don't Talk (Put Your Head on My Shoulder)">Don't Talk (Put Your Head on My Shoulder)</a>" sung by Brian Wilson.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-320"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-320">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Speculated by Mark Dillon to be <a href="/wiki/Tony_Asher" title="Tony Asher">Tony Asher</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon2012_319-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon2012-319">[281]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-214">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="extiw" href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Top_40-jaarlijst" title="nl:Top 40-jaarlijst">Top 40-jaarlijst</a>. The 'Top 40-jaarlijst' is the annual hit chart based on the week charts of the concerning year, resulting in the 'Hit van het Jaar'(Hit of the Year). It has been recorded ever since 1965.
